### 6.21 Message Count T144 Object

#### 6.21.1 INTRODUCTION

This object contains a count of the number of pending messages. This enables the host to use a direct memory access (DMA) transfer to read the messages from the Message Processor T5 object. See Section 3.2.1 "Introduction" for more information.

NOTE The mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family supports 2 active communications interfaces, so the Message Count T144 object maintains a message count and checksum for each interface independently. The Message Processor T5 object routes the messages to the appropriate interface, thus ensuring that each interface will receive its own messages.

#### 6.21.2 CONFIGURATION

TABLE 6-85: CONFIGURATION FOR MESSAGE COUNT T144

(SPT_MESSAGECOUNT_T144)

<table><tr><td>Byte</td><td>Field</td><td>Bit 7</td><td>Bit 6</td><td>Bit 5</td><td>Bit 4</td><td>Bit 3</td><td>Bit 2</td><td>Bit 1</td><td>Bit 0</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>COUNT</td><td colspan="8">Message count</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>CHECKSUM</td><td colspan="8">Checksum</td></tr></table>

## COUNT Field

This field specifies the number of pending messages that are ready to be sent.

## CHECKSUM Field

This field contains the 8-bit checksum for the Message Count T144 object.

See Appendix B "Checksum Calculation" for details on how to calculate the checksum.

mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family 1.0

### 6.22 Ignore Nodes Controller T145 Object

#### 6.22.1 INTRODUCTION

The Ignore Nodes Controller T145 object is used to specify how ignored nodes configured in Ignore Nodes T141 (see Section 5.16 "Ignore Nodes T141 Object") are applied to various measurement processes on the device. This may be useful for ignoring parts of the touchscreen that are not enabled for touch, such as with a free-form (non rectangular) screen shape, or when there are mechanical widgets present on the touchscreen.

There are currently two processes that Ignore Nodes T141 can be applied to:

- Touch Processing - Determined by CTRL IGNTCHPROCEN

- Self Test Control T10 Signal Limit Test - Determined by CTRL IGNSIGLIMEN

#### 6.22.2 CONFIGURATION

## TABLE 6-86: CONFIGURATION FOR IGNORE NODES CONTROLLER T145 (SPT_IGNORENODESCONTROL_T145)

<table><tr><td>Byte</td><td>Field</td><td>Bit 7</td><td>Bit 6</td><td>Bit 5</td><td>Bit 4</td><td>Bit 3</td><td>Bit 2</td><td>Bit 1</td><td>Bit 0</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>CTRL</td><td colspan="4">Reserved</td><td>IGNSIGLIMEN</td><td>IGNTCHPROCEN</td><td colspan="2">Reserved</td></tr></table>

## CTRL Field

IGNTCHPROCEN: When this bit is set to 1, any nodes configured by the Ignore Nodes T141 ignore node masks are not processed by any touchscreen touch or anti-touch processes. However, any mutual capacitance measurement data from nodes indicated by the Ignore Nodes T141 object will still be subject to processing by the following objects:

- Key Array T15 if configured as a Standard Key Array (note that Generic Key nodes are not bound by Ignore Nodes T141 ignore node masks)

- Lens Bending T65

- Noise Suppression T72

but will not be processed by the following:

- Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 and therefore any associated objects that process Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 touches (for example, Touch Suppression T42 Maximum Screen Area Suppression)

- Acquisition Configuration T8 calibration recovery

Care must be taken when using the Ignore Nodes T141 object. For a sensor design where ignored nodes are not physically present on the array (for example, a circular sensor design), Self Capacitance Touch measurements may be used, as long as the sensor tunes correctly (that is, the difference in load capacitance is within the Self Capacitance Global Configuration T109 tuning range of the device, or physical capacitors have been added).

For designs where the nodes are still physically present, but it is desired to suppress touch reporting or signal limit testing only in those areas (by setting YMASK bits in one or more instances of the Ignore Nodes T141 object for use on the Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 object), the associated instances of the following objects must be disabled:

- Retransmission Compensation T80

- Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104

- Self Capacitance Measurement Configuration T113

If Dual X is active, a node will be ignored in any measurement that includes that node if the node has been configured as an ignored node by Ignore Nodes T141. This applies to any of the processes listed above on objects that have the Dual X measurement capability (for example, Touchscreens, but not Key Arrays).

IGNSIGLIMEN: When this bit is set to 1, any nodes configured by the Ignore Nodes T141 ignore node masks are not tested by the Self Test Control T10 Mutual Capacitance Signal Limit Tests.

Note the following:

- The Ignore Nodes T141 instance representing the first X line used by a touchscreen (that is, X0) should contain at least one YMASK bit that is set to 0 . The reason is that this X line should have at least one normal (not ignored) node to ensure that the minimum and maximum values can be calculated for comparisons during the tests.

- If all the nodes for a touch object are configured as ignored nodes in Ignore Nodes T141, then the Self Test Control T10 Signal Limit Tests will fail for that touch object.

mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family 1.0

## APPENDIX A: MEASUREMENT PROCESSING

### A.1 Measurement Processing Objects

Figure A-1 shows the different measurement types on the mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family and the objects used to configure and process these.

## FIGURE A-1: MEASUREMENT PROCESSING

<img src="https://cdn.noedgeai.com/bo_d89ubaqlb0pc73cr34vg_261.jpg?x=152&y=517&w=1491&h=1516&r=0"/>

### A.2 Touch Detection

The mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family allows for both mutual and self capacitance measurements, with the self capacitance measurements being used to augment the mutual capacitance measurements to produce reliable touch information.

When self capacitance measurements are enabled, touch classification is achieved using both mutual and self capacitance touch data. This has the advantage that both types of measurement systems can work together to detect touches under a wide variety of circumstances.

Mutual capacitance touch data is used wherever possible to classify touches as this has greater granularity than self capacitance measurements and provides positional information on touches. For this reason, multiple touches can only be determined by mutual capacitance touch data. In Self Capacitance Touch Default mode, if the self capacitance touch processing detects multiple touches, touchscreen processing is skipped until mutual capacitance touch data is available.

Self capacitance measurements and P2P mutual capacitance measurements allow for the detection of single touches in extreme cases, such as single thick glove touches, when mutual capacitance touch detection alone may miss touches.

#### A.2.1 DEFAULT MEASUREMENTS

The system may be configured for different types of default measurements in idle and active modes.

For example, the device may be configured for Mutual Capacitance Touch Default Idle and Self Capacitance Touch Default Active (or any other combination; see Table A-1). Note that other types of scans (such as P2P mutual capacitance scans and other types of self capacitance scans) may also be made depending on configuration.

TABLE A-1: DEFAULT MEASUREMENT TYPES IN IDLE AND ACTIVE MODES

<table><tr><td>Mode</td><td>Measurement Type</td><td>Set By...</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3">Idle</td><td>Mutual Capacitance Touch</td><td>Automatically set as the default unless another measurement type is chosen as the default</td></tr><tr><td>Self Capacitance Touch</td><td>Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104 CTRL DEFMEASIDLE control</td></tr><tr><td>Self Capacitance Single-ended</td><td>Automatically measured if Self Capacitance Measurement Configuration T113 is enabled. Explicitly used for Low Power Idle mode if CTRL DEFMEASIDLE = 1.</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">Active</td><td>Mutual Capacitance Touch</td><td>Automatically set as the default unless another measurement type is chosen as the default</td></tr><tr><td>Self Capacitance Touch</td><td>Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104 CTRL DEFMEASACTV control</td></tr></table>

### A.3 Touch Classification

#### A.3.1 FINGER TOUCHES

Finger touches are classified when the following conditions are met:

- Mutual capacitance touch delta ≥ Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 TCHTHR, or

mutual capacitance touch deltas < Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 TCHTHR

but has been compensated by Retransmission Compensation T80

and (if Self Capacitance Touch Default Active mode)

- Self capacitance touch deltas \( \geq \) Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104 XTCHTHR/YTCHTHR

#### A.3.2 GLOVE TOUCHES

Normal thin glove touches are classified using mutual capacitance data when the following conditions are met:

- Mutual capacitance touch deltas ≥ Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 INTTHR

and

mutual capacitance touch deltas < Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 TCHTHR (out of detect)

and

- The conditions set in Glove Detection T78 are met for glove touches

and

- (If Mutual Capacitance touch deltas are not present on a cycle)

Self capacitance touch deltas \( \geq \) Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104 XINTTHR/YINTTHR

Note that multiple touch glove touches are supported (mutual measurements only).

Thick glove touches are classified using self capacitance data when the following conditions are met:

- Self capacitance touch deltas \( \geq \) Glove Detection T78 SCTGLOVETHR and meet Glove Detection T78 criteria

and

- (If Mutual Capacitance touch deltas are present on a cycle)

Mutual capacitance touch delta < Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 INTTHR

#### A.3.3 PASSIVE STYLUS TOUCHES

Stylus touches are classified when the following conditions are met:

- Mutual capacitance touch deltas \( \geq \) Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 INTTHR and

mutual capacitance touch deltas < Multiple Touch Touchscreen T100 TCHTHR

and

- The conditions set in the Passive Stylus T47 object are met for stylus touches and (if Self Capacitance Touch Default Active mode)

- Self capacitance touch deltas ≥ Auxiliary Touch Configuration T104 XINTTHR/YINTTHR

#### A.3.4 SUPPRESSED LARGE TOUCHES

Suppressed large touches are classified when the following conditions are met:

- Touch Suppression T42 has detected a large touch for suppression

and

- Touch Suppression T42 CFG SUPTCHRPTEN bit is set to 1 to allow the reporting of suppressed large touches

## APPENDIX B: CHECKSUM CALCULATION

### B.1 24-bit CRC

#### B.1.1 INTRODUCTION

The device uses a 24-bit cyclic redundancy check (CRC) in the following places:

- To check the integrity of the main Information Block for the device

- To check the integrity of the Information Block T254 object

- To check the integrity of the configuration settings held in the non-volatile memory

These CRC checksums allow the host to be confident that the memory map layout has been read over the communications bus correctly.

The CRC polynomial for a 24-bit CRC is 0x80001B and the initial CRC value is 0x0000.

NOTE The C code in this appendix uses the type declarations uint8_t, uint16_t and uint32_t for 8-bit, 16-bit and 32-bit unsigned integers respectively.

#### B.1.2 24-BIT CRC ALGORITHM

Each checksum is generated by running an algorithm which takes two new bytes of data and combines them with the current checksum to produce a new checksum value.

The sample code below shows how to calculate the checksum for each 16-bit word in a byte stream.

---

uint32_t crc24(uint32_t crc, uint8_t firstbyte, uint8_t secondbyte)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;static const uint32_t CRCPOLY = 0x80001B;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;uint36_t result;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;uint16_t data_word;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;data_word = (uint16_t)firstbyte | (uint16_t)( (uint16_t)secondbyte << 8u);

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;result = (uint32_t)data_word ^ (uint32_t)(crc << 1u);

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;if (result & 0x1000000) // If bit 25 is set

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;result \( {}^{\kappa } = \) CRCPOLY;// XOR result with CRCPOLY

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\}

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return result;

\}

---

The checksum routine is called iteratively to calculate the CRC two bytes (16-bit word) at a time. This means that two bytes must be read from the device before performing each iteration of the checksum. Therefore, if an odd number of bytes is read from the device, the host's checksum code should add a zero byte to the end of the byte stream to make the sequence even. For example, if the following stream of 7 bytes is received from the device:

byte1 - byte2 - byte3 - byte4 - byte5 - byte6 - byte7

The checksum could be calculated as follows:

---

uint32_t CRC = 0;

CRC = crc24(CRC, byte1, byte2)

CRC = crc24(CRC, byte3, byte4)

CRC = crc24(CRC, byte5, byte6)

CRC = Crc24(CRC, byte7, 0) 	/* <- zero added for the last checksum */

CRC = CRC & 0x00FFFFFF; 	/* <- mask the 32-bit result to 24-bit */

---

An alternative method of calling the CRC calculation function is to use a loop, as shown in Section B.1.3 "Information Block Checksum".

Table B-1 shows an example block of 32 bytes and the CRCs these generate. The bytes consist of 31 data bytes plus an additional zero byte to even up the count. You can use the data in this table to verify the validity of any coded CRC routine.

TABLE B-1: EXAMPLE CRC CALCULATIONS FOR 24-BIT CRC

<table><tr><td>First Byte</td><td>Second Byte</td><td colspan="2">CRC Calculations</td></tr><tr><td>0x00</td><td>0xFF</td><td>0x00FF00</td><td rowspan="15">Intermediate CRC (partial calculation)</td></tr><tr><td>0x11</td><td>0xEE</td><td>0x011011</td></tr><tr><td>0x22</td><td>0xDD</td><td>0x02FD00</td></tr><tr><td>0x33</td><td>0xCC</td><td>0x053633</td></tr><tr><td>0x44</td><td>0xBB</td><td>0x0AD722</td></tr><tr><td>0x55</td><td>0xAA</td><td>0x150411</td></tr><tr><td>0x66</td><td>0x99</td><td>0x2A9144</td></tr><tr><td>0x77</td><td>0x88</td><td>0x55AAFF</td></tr><tr><td>0x88</td><td>0x77</td><td>0xAB2276</td></tr><tr><td>0x99</td><td>0x65</td><td>0xD6226E</td></tr><tr><td>0xAA</td><td>0x54</td><td>0x2C116D</td></tr><tr><td>0xBB</td><td>0x44</td><td>0x586661</td></tr><tr><td>0xCC</td><td>0x33</td><td>0xB0FF0E</td></tr><tr><td>0xDD</td><td>0x22</td><td>0xE1DCDA</td></tr><tr><td>0xEE</td><td>0x11</td><td>0x43A841</td></tr><tr><td>0xFF</td><td>0x00</td><td>0x87507D</td><td>Expected CRC (final calculation)</td></tr></table>

#### B.1.3 INFORMATION BLOCK CHECKSUM

The checksum for the Information Block should be calculated by the host on start-up when the Information Block is first read. This should be compared to the checksum value at the end of the Information Block. If there is a mismatch, an error has occurred.

The following code shows how to use the example crc24 () function given in Section B.1.2 "24-bit CRC Algorithm" to calculate the CRC checksum for the Information Block.

---

uint32_t crc = 0; 													/* Calculated CRC */

uint16_t crc_area_size; 													/* Size of data for CRC calculation */

uint8_t *mem; 													/* Data buffer */

uint8_t is

uint8_t status;

/* 7 bytes of version data, 6 * NUM_OF_OBJECTS bytes of object table. */

crc_area_size = ID_INFORMATION_SIZE +

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;info_block->info_id.num_declared_objects *

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;OBJECT_TABLE_ELEMENT_SIZE;

mem = (uint8_t *) malloc(crc_area_size);

if (mem == NULL)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;/* Handle error */

\}

/*

* Read the data using a function written for this purpose

* Here, it is assumed that the function is named read_mem()

* and that it returns a status code with the value READ_MEM_OK.

* It is assumed to have the following prototype:

* uint8_t read_mem( uint16_t Address, uint8_t ByteCount, uint8_t *Data )

*/

status = read_mem(0, crc_area_size, mem);

if (status != READ_MEM_OK)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;/* Handle error */

\}

/*

* Call the CRC function crc24() iteratively to calculate the CRC,

* passing it two bytes at a time.

*/

i = 0;

while (i < (crc_area_size - 1))

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;crc = crc24(crc, *(mem + i), *(mem + i + 1));

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;i += 2;

\}

/*

* Call the crc24() with the final byte,

* plus an extra 0 value byte to make the sequence even.

*/

crc = crc24(crc, *(mem + i), 0);

free (mem);

/* Final result */

Crc = (Crc & 0x00FFFFFF);

---

/* <- mask the 32-bit result to 24-bit */

#### B.1.4 CONFIGURATION CHECKSUM

The configuration checksum checks the integrity of the memory map whenever a write to the device non-volatile memory takes place. Typically this is when the device is initially set in the factory or during subsequent firmware upgrades. The host should perform a CRC on the entire contents of the memory map from the address of the Key Thresholds T14 object onwards. This CRC should then be compared with the expected checksum.

### B.2 8-bit CRC

#### B.2.1 INTRODUCTION

In order to comply with UL/IEC 60730 Class B, an 8-bit CRC must be used in the following places:

- When reading message data from the device, to check the integrity of data in messages from the Message Processor T5 object (see Section B.2.4 "Reading Message Processor T5 Messages with an 8-bit CRC").

- When reading the Message Count T144 message count during DMA access.

- When performing a block read of Data Container T117 data, the Data Container Controller T118 object can request an 8-bit CRC for certain types of data. See Data Container Controller T118 "HEADERTYPE Field" for more information.

- When writing data to the device, to check that data has been transmitted by the host over the communications bus correctly (see Section B.2.3 "Writing to the device with an 8-bit CRC").

The CRC polynomial for an 8-bit CRC is 0x8C and the initial CRC value is 0x00.

B.2.2 8-BIT CRC ALGORITHM

The sample code below shows how to calculate the 8-bit checksum.

---

uint8_t crc8(uint8_t crc, uint8_t data)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;static const uint8_t CRCPOLY = 0x8C;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;for (uint8_t index = 0; index < 8; index++)

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;uint8_t fb = (crc ^ data) & 0x01;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;data >>> 1;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;crc >>= 1;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;if (fb) \{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Crc ^= CRCPOLY;

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\}

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;\}

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return crc;

\}

---

B.2.3 WRITING TO THE DEVICE WITH AN 8-BIT CRC

When writing to the device, an 8-bit CRC must be added to all data transmissions from the host. The CRC is calculated on all the bytes sent, including the address bytes, but excluding the SLA+W byte.

B.2.3.1 Example Write Operation with an 8-bit CRC

The examples in Figure B-1 and Figure B-2 set the address pointer to 0x1234.

An extra byte that holds the Data checksum is sent as the last byte. The device compares this checksum with its own checksum calculated from the data received. If the two checksums do not match, the Command Processor T6 object in the device generates a COMSERR message (see Section 3.3.3 "Messages").

NOTE The order of messages is not guaranteed so messages may be out of order. Other intervening messages may be received before the COMSERR message.

The following code shows how to use the example crc8 () function given in Section B.2.2 "8-bit CRC Algorithm" to

calculate the CRC checksum for the data in the Message Processor T5 object.

uint8_t crc = 0;

uint8_t data_in;

for (i = 0; i<MESSAGEPROCESSOR_SIZE; i++)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;data_in = read_byte();

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;crc = crc8(crc, data_in);

\}

if(crc == 0)

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;/* CRC is OK - do something appropriate */

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;crc_pass();

\}

else

\{

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;/* CRC failed - handle error */

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;crc_fail();

\}

---

## APPENDIX C: BOOTLOADING PROCEDURE

The maXTouch devices have a common bootloading procedure that allows them to be forced into a special application update mode of operation.

For more information on the bootloading procedure, refer to the following application note:

- Application Note: MXTAN0216 - Bootloading Procedure for maXTouch Devices

### C.1 Bootloader ID

The bootloader ID for the mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family is 0xB9. The mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family bootloader uses extended ID mode.

### C.2 \( {\mathrm{I}}^{2}\mathrm{C} \) address

The \( {\mathrm{I}}^{2}\mathrm{C} \) address for bootloader mode on the mXT640UD-CCUBHA1 Family is 0x27.

## APPENDIX E: FUTURE INFORMATION BLOCK T254 OBJECT

### E.1 Introduction

Currently the Object Table entries are held within the Information Block for the device. The current format, however, limits the object type codes to 8 bits. It is anticipated that at some time in the future new objects will be issued with 16-bit type codes (that is, codes above 255). The Information Block T254 object will therefore be added to the protocol to accommodate Object Table elements with 16-bit type codes. This means that if the Information Block T254 object is present on a device, it must be checked for any further Object Table entries.

NOTE It is important for future compatibility that any current driver code is written to allow for the presence of the Information Block T254 object when it is eventually used. The host driver code must parse the Object Table, as at present, and also process the Information Block T254 object if it is found to be present on the device.

This appendix gives the format of the future Information Block T254 object so that the driver code can be written accordingly.

### E.2 Information Block T254

This object acts as an extension to the Information Block at the start of the device's memory map and holds additional Object Table entries. Specifically, it hold entries for those objects that have 16-bit type codes (that is, type codes above 255).

TABLE 6-87: CONFIGURATION FOR INFORMATION BLOCK T254 (GEN_INFOBLOCK16BIT_T254)

<table><tr><td>Byte</td><td>Field</td><td>Bit 7</td><td>Bit 6</td><td>Bit 5</td><td>Bit 4</td><td>Bit 3</td><td>Bit 2</td><td>Bit 1</td><td>Bit 0</td></tr><tr><td>0-6</td><td>Object Table</td><td colspan="8">Object Table element 1 (7 bytes)</td></tr><tr><td>7-13</td><td>Object Table</td><td colspan="8">Object Table element 2 (7 bytes)</td></tr><tr><td colspan="10">...</td></tr><tr><td>m*7 – (m*7)+6</td><td>Object Table</td><td colspan="8">Last Object Table element (7 bytes)</td></tr><tr><td>(m+1)*7 –</td><td>Checksum</td><td colspan="8" rowspan="2">24-bit checksum <br> (3 bytes)</td></tr><tr><td>(m+1)*7+2</td><td></td></tr></table>

## Object Table Element Fields

These 7-byte fields hold the additional elements in the Object Table. The format of each element is similar to that of the Object Table elements held directly in the main Object Table for the device (see Section 1.5 "Object Table"), except that the type code occupies two bytes instead of one. The format of each element is given in Table E-1.

TABLE E-1: OBJECT TABLE ELEMENT FORMAT

<table><tr><td>Byte</td><td>Description</td></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>Type code LSByte</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>Type code MSByte</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>Start position LSByte</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>Start position MSByte</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>Size – 1</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>Instances – 1</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>Number of Report IDs per instance</td></tr></table>

Type code (2 bytes): This 16-bit field holds the unique type code for the object to identify it. This is the number after the "_T" suffix at the end of the object's internal name as given in the object descriptions. For example, the type code for the Command Processor T6 is 6 (from GEN_COMMANDPROCESSOR_T6).

Start position (2 bytes): This 16-bit field holds the start location of the object in the memory map.

Size - 1 (1 byte): This field holds the size (minus 1) of the object in the memory map. This is stored as Size-1, so it is effectively the offset to the end of the object.

Instances - 1 (1 byte): This holds the number of instances (minus 1) of the object in the memory map. The number of instances can be calculated by adding 1 to this number (see Section 1.6.2 "Object Instances"). The different instances of an object are arranged consecutively in the memory map.

Number of Report IDs per instance (1 byte): This field holds the number of report IDs for each instance of the object. See Section 1.5.6 "Report IDs"for more information on report IDs.

## Checksum Field

A checksum of the contents of the Information Block T254 object. This allows the host to check that the Object Table elements stored in the Information Block T254 object have been read correctly over the communications interface. See Appendix B "Checksum Calculation" for details on calculating the checksum.

## THE MICROCHIP WEB SITE

Microchip provides online support via our WWW site at www.microchip.com. This web site is used as a means to make files and information easily available to customers. Accessible by using your favorite Internet browser, the web site contains the following information:

- Product Support - Datasheets and errata, application notes and sample programs, design resources, user's guides and hardware support documents, latest software releases and archived software

- General Technical Support - Frequently Asked Questions (FAQ), technical support requests, online discussion groups, Microchip consultant program member listing

- Business of Microchip - Product selector and ordering guides, latest Microchip press releases, listing of seminars and events, listings of Microchip sales offices, distributors and factory representatives

## THE MAXTOUCH WEB SITE

Information on Microchip's maXTouch product line can be accessed via the microchip web site at www.microchip.com, This information is also available for direct access via a short-cut at www.maxtouch.com. The maXTouch web pages contain the following information:

- Product Information - Product specifications, brochures, datasheets, protocol guides

- Tools and Software - Evaluation kits, maXTouch Studio, software libraries for individual maXTouch touch controllers

- Training and Support - Generic application notes and training material for the maXTouch product range

## CUSTOMER CHANGE NOTIFICATION SERVICE

Microchip's customer notification service helps keep customers current on Microchip products. Subscribers will receive e-mail notification whenever there are changes, updates, revisions or errata related to a specified product family or development tool of interest.

To register, access the Microchip web site at www.microchip.com. Under "Support", click on "Customer Change Notification" and follow the registration instructions.

## CUSTOMER SUPPORT

Users of Microchip products can receive assistance through several channels:

- Distributor or Representative

- Local Sales Office

- Field Application Engineer (FAE)

- Technical Support

Customers should contact their distributor, representative or Field Application Engineer (FAE) for support. Local sales offices are also available to help customers. A listing of sales offices and locations is included in the back of this document.

Technical support is available through the web site at: http://microchip.com/support

## Note the following details of the code protection feature on Microchip products:

- Microchip products meet the specifications contained in their particular Microchip Data Sheet.

- Microchip believes that its family of products is secure when used in the intended manner, within operating specifications, and under normal conditions.

- Microchip values and aggressively protects its intellectual property rights. Attempts to breach the code protection features of Microchip products is strictly prohibited and may violate the Digital Millennium Copyright Act.

- Neither Microchip nor any other semiconductor manufacturer can guarantee the security of its code. Code protection does not mean that we are guaranteeing the product is "unbreakable". Code protection is constantly evolving. Microchip is committed to continuously improving the code protection features of our products.
